## Chapter: Chapter 11: Viscoelasticity

### Introduction

Viscoelasticity is a fascinating and complex field that bridges the gap between solid mechanics and fluid dynamics. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of viscoelasticity, providing a comprehensive guide to understanding and applying its principles in the field of computational mechanics of materials.

Viscoelastic materials exhibit both viscous and elastic properties. They deform under stress, much like an elastic material, but their deformation also depends on the rate at which the stress is applied, much like a viscous fluid. This unique behavior is observed in a wide range of materials, from biological tissues to synthetic polymers, and understanding it is crucial for predicting their response to external forces.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe viscoelastic behavior. These models, often expressed in terms of differential equations, allow us to predict the response of viscoelastic materials to various loading conditions. We will also discuss the numerical methods used to solve these equations, providing a practical approach to the study of viscoelasticity.

We will also delve into the concept of viscoelasticity in the context of computational mechanics. This includes the use of computational models to simulate the behavior of viscoelastic materials under various loading conditions. These models, which often involve the use of finite element methods, allow us to predict the response of viscoelastic materials to complex loading conditions that would be difficult to analyze using traditional analytical methods.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the principles of viscoelasticity and be able to apply these principles in the field of computational mechanics of materials. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field, this chapter will provide you with the tools you need to understand and apply the principles of viscoelasticity.




#### 10.4b Anisotropic Assumed Strain Methods

Anisotropic assumed strain methods are a class of numerical techniques used in computational mechanics to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. These methods are particularly useful in cases where the material properties vary significantly in different directions, such as in the case of crystalline materials.

The basic idea behind anisotropic assumed strain methods is to approximate the strain in the material by a set of assumed strains. These assumed strains are then used to calculate the stress in the material, which can be compared with the applied stress to solve the problem.

One of the most common anisotropic assumed strain methods is the gradient discretisation method (GDM). The GDM is a numerical method that satisfies several key properties, including coercivity, GD-consistency, limit-conformity, compactness, and piecewise constant reconstruction.

The GDM can be implemented using various numerical methods, including Galerkin methods and conforming finite element methods. In the case of the Galerkin method, the GDM is identical to the method where one defines the space $V_h$ spanned by the finite basis $(\psi_i)_{i\in I}$, and the constant $C_D$ involved in the continuous Poincaré inequality, and $W_{D}(\varphi) = 0$ for all $\varphi\in H_\operatorname{div}(\Omega)$.

The "mass-lumped" $P^1$ finite element case can also be used in the GDM, replacing $\Pi_D u$ by $\widetilde{\Pi}_D u = \sum_{i\in I} u_i \chi_{\Omega_i}$, where $\Omega_i$ is a dual cell centred on the vertex indexed by $i\in I$. This allows for the piecewise constant reconstruction property.

Another important anisotropic assumed strain method is the nonconforming finite element method. This method is particularly useful in cases where the material properties vary significantly in different directions, and it can be used to approximate the solution of the problem.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the application of these anisotropic assumed strain methods in the context of the equations of anisotropic elasticity.

#### 10.4c Applications and Examples

In this section, we will explore some practical applications and examples of anisotropic assumed strain methods. These methods are particularly useful in the field of computational mechanics, where they are used to solve complex problems involving anisotropic materials.

##### Example 1: Stress Analysis in a Crystalline Material

Consider a crystalline material subjected to a certain load. The material properties of the crystal are anisotropic, meaning they vary significantly in different directions. To solve for the stress in the material, we can use the gradient discretisation method (GDM) with the Galerkin method.

The GDM is implemented by defining the space $V_h$ spanned by the finite basis $(\psi_i)_{i\in I}$, and the constant $C_D$ involved in the continuous Poincaré inequality. The assumed strain is then calculated, and the stress is determined by comparing it with the applied stress.

##### Example 2: Finite Element Analysis of a Composite Material

Another application of anisotropic assumed strain methods is in the finite element analysis of composite materials. Composite materials are made up of two or more constituent materials with different properties, and their properties can vary significantly in different directions.

The GDM can be used to solve for the stress in the composite material. The method is implemented by defining the space $V_h$ and the constant $C_D$, as in the previous example. The assumed strain is then calculated, and the stress is determined by comparing it with the applied stress.

##### Example 3: Nonconforming Finite Element Method in a Complex Structure

The nonconforming finite element method is particularly useful in cases where the material properties vary significantly in different directions, and the structure is complex. This method can be used to solve for the stress in the structure by defining the nonconforming finite elements and the constant $C_D$.

The assumed strain is then calculated, and the stress is determined by comparing it with the applied stress. The piecewise constant reconstruction property of the GDM is particularly useful in this case, as it allows for the accurate calculation of the stress in the complex structure.

In conclusion, anisotropic assumed strain methods are powerful tools in computational mechanics. They allow for the accurate calculation of stress in complex structures and materials, and their applications are vast and varied.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the realm of assumed strain methods, a crucial aspect of computational mechanics of materials. We have explored the theoretical underpinnings of these methods, their applications, and the benefits they offer in the field of material mechanics. 

Assumed strain methods, as we have seen, provide a simplified approach to solving complex problems in material mechanics. By making certain assumptions about the strain distribution, these methods allow us to solve problems that would otherwise be intractable. However, it is important to note that these methods are based on assumptions, and their accuracy depends on the validity of these assumptions.

In the realm of computational mechanics, assumed strain methods are particularly useful. They allow us to solve problems that involve large deformations, non-linear material behavior, and complex geometries. They also provide a means to incorporate material properties and boundary conditions in a systematic manner.

In conclusion, assumed strain methods are a powerful tool in the field of computational mechanics of materials. They provide a systematic approach to solving complex problems, and their applications are vast. However, it is important to remember that these methods are based on assumptions, and their accuracy depends on the validity of these assumptions.

#### 10.5a Overview of Mixed Methods

Mixed methods in computational mechanics of materials are a combination of different numerical techniques used to solve complex problems. These methods are particularly useful when dealing with problems that involve both continuous and discrete variables, or when the material properties vary significantly in different directions.

One of the most common mixed methods is the finite element method (FEM). The FEM is a numerical method that discretises the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solves the problem within each element using a set of basis functions. The solution within each element is then assembled to form the overall solution.

The FEM can be combined with other methods, such as the assumed strain methods, to form a mixed method. For example, the gradient discretisation method (GDM) can be combined with the FEM to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FEM is used to solve the problem within each element.

Another important mixed method is the finite volume method (FVM). The FVM is a numerical method that discretises the problem domain into a finite number of volumes, and then solves the problem within each volume using a set of basis functions. The solution within each volume is then assembled to form the overall solution.

The FVM can also be combined with other methods, such as the anisotropic assumed strain methods, to form a mixed method. For example, the GDM can be combined with the FVM to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FVM is used to solve the problem within each volume.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these mixed methods and explore their applications in solving complex problems in computational mechanics of materials.

#### 10.5b Implementation of Mixed Methods

Implementing mixed methods in computational mechanics of materials involves a careful combination of different numerical techniques. The implementation process can be broken down into several steps, each of which requires careful consideration.

1. **Problem Formulation:** The first step in implementing a mixed method is to formulate the problem. This involves identifying the problem domain, the variables involved, and the equations that govern the problem. For example, in the case of the QUIS Version 5.0, the problem domain was the interface of a software product, the variables were the user's ratings and comments, and the equations were those governing user satisfaction.

2. **Discretisation:** The next step is to discretise the problem domain. This involves dividing the problem domain into a finite number of elements or volumes, depending on the chosen method. For example, in the QUIS Version 5.0, the interface was divided into four groups, each of which was rated by a different group of participants.

3. **Solution Method Selection:** Once the problem domain has been discretised, the appropriate solution method must be selected. This involves choosing the numerical technique that best suits the problem at hand. For example, in the QUIS Version 5.0, the researchers used a combination of the finite element method (FEM) and the gradient discretisation method (GDM) to solve the problem.

4. **Implementation of Solution Method:** The selected solution method must then be implemented. This involves coding the necessary algorithms and equations, and testing the implementation to ensure that it is functioning correctly. For example, in the QUIS Version 5.0, the researchers used a combination of the FEM and the GDM to solve the problem. The FEM was used to solve the problem within each group, while the GDM was used to approximate the strain in the interface.

5. **Validation and Verification:** The final step is to validate and verify the implementation. This involves checking that the implementation is producing correct results, and comparing these results with known solutions or experimental data. For example, in the QUIS Version 5.0, the researchers validated and verified their implementation by comparing the results with those of a previous study using the QUIS Version 4.0.

In conclusion, implementing mixed methods in computational mechanics of materials is a complex process that requires careful consideration at each step. However, with careful planning and testing, these methods can provide powerful tools for solving complex problems.

#### 10.5c Applications and Examples

Mixed methods have been widely used in various fields, including software engineering, user interface design, and materials science. In this section, we will explore some examples of how mixed methods have been applied in these fields.

##### Software Engineering

In software engineering, mixed methods have been used to evaluate the user satisfaction of software products. For instance, the QUIS Version 5.0, a questionnaire for user interface satisfaction, was used to evaluate the satisfaction of users of a local PC User's Group. The researchers used a combination of the finite element method (FEM) and the gradient discretisation method (GDM) to solve the problem. The FEM was used to solve the problem within each group, while the GDM was used to approximate the strain in the interface. The results of the study showed that the overall reliability of QUIS Version 5.0 using Cronbach's alpha was .939.

##### User Interface Design

Mixed methods have also been used in user interface design. For example, the QUIS Version 5.5, an improved version of the QUIS Version 5.0, was used to evaluate the user satisfaction of a software product. The researchers used a combination of the FEM and the GDM to solve the problem. The FEM was used to solve the problem within each group, while the GDM was used to approximate the strain in the interface. The output format of the QUIS data was also improved to make analysis easier.

##### Materials Science

In materials science, mixed methods have been used to study the mechanical properties of materials. For instance, the finite volume method (FVM) was used to study the mechanical properties of a material under different loading conditions. The FVM was used to solve the problem within each volume, while the GDM was used to approximate the strain in the material. The results of the study showed that the FVM and the GDM provided a powerful tool for studying the mechanical properties of materials.

In conclusion, mixed methods have proven to be a powerful tool in various fields. They allow for the combination of different numerical techniques to solve complex problems. The examples provided in this section illustrate the versatility and power of mixed methods.

Assumed strain methods, as we have seen, provide a simplified approach to the analysis of materials, particularly in complex scenarios where the material properties are not constant. By assuming a certain strain distribution, we can simplify the problem and solve it more easily. This approach is particularly useful in the analysis of non-uniform materials, where the properties vary significantly across the material.

However, it is important to note that assumed strain methods are not without their limitations. They are based on certain assumptions, and if these assumptions are not met, the results may not be accurate. Therefore, it is crucial to understand the limitations of these methods and to use them appropriately.

#### 10.5b Mixed Formulations for Elasticity

Mixed formulations for elasticity are a powerful tool in computational mechanics of materials. They allow us to solve complex problems involving anisotropic materials, non-convex domains, and varying material properties. In this section, we will explore the implementation of mixed formulations for elasticity, focusing on the finite element method (FEM) and the finite volume method (FVM).

##### Finite Element Method

The FEM is a numerical method that discretises the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solves the problem within each element using a set of basis functions. The solution within each element is then assembled to form the overall solution.

In the context of mixed formulations for elasticity, the FEM can be combined with the gradient discretisation method (GDM) to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FEM is used to solve the problem within each element.

The implementation of the FEM involves discretising the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solving the system of equations that represents the problem within each element. This is typically done using a numerical solver, such as the Newton-Raphson method.

##### Finite Volume Method

The FVM is a numerical method that discretises the problem domain into a finite number of volumes, and then solves the problem within each volume using a set of basis functions. The solution within each volume is then assembled to form the overall solution.

In the context of mixed formulations for elasticity, the FVM can be combined with the GDM to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FVM is used to solve the problem within each volume.

The implementation of the FVM involves discretising the problem domain into a finite number of volumes, and then solving the system of equations that represents the problem within each volume. This is typically done using a numerical solver, such as the Newton-Raphson method.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the specifics of implementing these mixed methods, focusing on the numerical techniques used to solve the system of equations that represents the problem.

#### 10.5c Applications and Examples

In this section, we will explore some practical applications and examples of mixed methods in computational mechanics of materials. We will focus on the implementation of these methods in the context of elasticity problems.

##### Example 1: Anisotropic Material under Tension

Consider an anisotropic material under tension. The material properties vary in the direction of the applied force, and the material is subject to a non-convex domain. This is a complex problem that can be solved using mixed methods.

We can implement the FEM and FVM in conjunction with the GDM to solve this problem. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FEM and FVM are used to solve the problem within each element and volume, respectively.

The implementation involves discretising the problem domain into a finite number of elements and volumes, and then solving the system of equations that represents the problem within each element and volume. This is typically done using a numerical solver, such as the Newton-Raphson method.

##### Example 2: Non-Convex Domain under Compression

Consider a non-convex domain under compression. The material properties are constant, but the domain is non-convex, making the problem complex.

Again, we can implement the FEM and FVM in conjunction with the GDM to solve this problem. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FEM and FVM are used to solve the problem within each element and volume, respectively.

The implementation involves discretising the problem domain into a finite number of elements and volumes, and then solving the system of equations that represents the problem within each element and volume. This is typically done using a numerical solver, such as the Newton-Raphson method.

These examples illustrate the power and versatility of mixed methods in solving complex problems in computational mechanics of materials. By combining different numerical methods, we can tackle problems that would be difficult or impossible to solve using a single method.




#### 10.6a Discretization Techniques in Lagrangian Formulation

The Lagrangian formulation is a powerful tool in computational mechanics of materials, particularly in the context of assumed strain methods. It allows us to discretize the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solve the problem within each element using a set of basis functions. The solution within each element is then assembled to form the overall solution.

##### Finite Element Method

The Finite Element Method (FEM) is a numerical method that discretises the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solves the problem within each element using a set of basis functions. The solution within each element is then assembled to form the overall solution.

In the context of the Lagrangian formulation, the FEM can be combined with the gradient discretisation method (GDM) to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FEM is used to solve the problem within each element.

The implementation of the FEM involves discretising the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solving the system of equations that represents the problem within each element. This is typically done using a numerical solver, such as the Newton-Raphson method.

##### Finite Volume Method

The Finite Volume Method (FVM) is a numerical method that discretises the problem domain into a finite number of volumes, and then solves the problem within each volume using a set of basis functions. The solution within each volume is then assembled to form the overall solution.

In the context of the Lagrangian formulation, the FVM can be combined with the GDM to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FVM is used to solve the problem within each volume.

The implementation of the FVM involves discretising the problem domain into a finite number of volumes, and then solving the system of equations that represents the problem within each volume. This is typically done using a numerical solver, such as the Newton-Raphson method.

##### Mesh Refinement Techniques

Mesh refinement techniques are used to improve the accuracy of the solution by increasing the number of elements or volumes in the problem domain. This is particularly useful in regions where the solution changes rapidly, or where high accuracy is required.

In the context of the Lagrangian formulation, mesh refinement techniques can be used in conjunction with the FEM or FVM to improve the accuracy of the solution. This is typically done by defining a refinement criterion, such as a maximum allowable error or a minimum element size, and then refining the mesh accordingly.

The implementation of mesh refinement techniques involves defining the refinement criterion, and then using a mesh generator to create the refined mesh. The solution is then solved on the refined mesh, and the process is repeated until the refinement criterion is met.

#### 10.6b Applications and Examples

The discretized Lagrangian formulation is a powerful tool in computational mechanics of materials, with a wide range of applications. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and provide examples to illustrate the use of the discretized Lagrangian formulation in practice.

##### Structural Analysis

One of the primary applications of the discretized Lagrangian formulation is in structural analysis. The Finite Element Method (FEM) and Finite Volume Method (FVM) can be used to solve problems involving anisotropic materials, such as composites or metals, by discretizing the problem domain into a finite number of elements or volumes.

For example, consider a composite beam subjected to a bending moment. The beam can be discretized into a finite number of elements, and the problem can be solved using the FEM or FVM. The solution provides the displacement and stress distribution within the beam, which can be used to assess the structural integrity of the beam.

##### Fluid Dynamics

The discretized Lagrangian formulation can also be used in fluid dynamics. The FEM and FVM can be combined with the gradient discretisation method (GDM) to solve problems involving anisotropic materials, such as viscous fluids.

For example, consider a viscous fluid flowing through a pipe. The pipe and the fluid can be discretized into a finite number of elements and volumes, respectively. The problem can be solved using the FEM or FVM, and the solution provides the velocity and pressure distribution within the fluid.

##### Heat Transfer

The discretized Lagrangian formulation can be used in heat transfer problems. The FEM and FVM can be combined with the GDM to solve problems involving anisotropic materials, such as heat-conducting solids.

For example, consider a heat-conducting solid subjected to a temperature gradient. The solid can be discretized into a finite number of elements, and the problem can be solved using the FEM or FVM. The solution provides the temperature distribution within the solid, which can be used to assess the thermal integrity of the solid.

In conclusion, the discretized Lagrangian formulation is a versatile tool in computational mechanics of materials, with a wide range of applications. The Finite Element Method and Finite Volume Method, combined with the gradient discretisation method, provide a powerful framework for solving complex problems involving anisotropic materials.

#### 10.6c Limitations and Future Directions

While the discretized Lagrangian formulation has proven to be a powerful tool in computational mechanics of materials, it is not without its limitations. These limitations often provide opportunities for further research and development.

##### Complex Geometries

One of the main limitations of the discretized Lagrangian formulation is its difficulty in handling complex geometries. The discretization process can be challenging when dealing with irregular shapes or multiple connected components. This can lead to inaccuracies in the solution, particularly in problems involving anisotropic materials.

Future research in this area could focus on developing more efficient and accurate methods for discretizing complex geometries. This could involve the use of advanced numerical techniques, such as mesh adaptation or level set methods.

##### Non-linear Material Behavior

Another limitation of the discretized Lagrangian formulation is its reliance on linear material behavior. Many real-world materials, such as composites or metals, exhibit non-linear behavior under loading. This can lead to inaccuracies in the solution, particularly in problems involving large deformations.

Future research in this area could focus on developing more accurate and efficient methods for handling non-linear material behavior. This could involve the use of advanced constitutive models, such as strain energy density functions or co-rotational formulations.

##### High Computational Cost

The discretized Lagrangian formulation can be computationally intensive, particularly for large-scale problems. This can limit its applicability in real-world engineering problems, where computational resources may be limited.

Future research in this area could focus on developing more efficient numerical methods, such as parallel computing or adaptive time-stepping. This could also involve the development of more efficient numerical solvers, such as quasi-Newton methods or trust region methods.

In conclusion, while the discretized Lagrangian formulation has proven to be a powerful tool in computational mechanics of materials, there are still many opportunities for further research and development. By addressing these limitations, we can continue to improve and expand the capabilities of this powerful computational tool.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the assumed strain methods, a crucial aspect of computational mechanics of materials. We have explored the theoretical underpinnings of these methods, their applications, and the advantages they offer in the field of material mechanics. 

The assumed strain methods, as we have seen, provide a simplified approach to solving complex problems in material mechanics. By assuming certain strains, we can reduce the complexity of the problem and solve it more efficiently. This approach is particularly useful in cases where the material behavior is non-linear or when the loading conditions are complex.

However, it is important to note that the assumed strain methods are based on certain assumptions and approximations. These assumptions may not always hold true, and therefore, the results obtained from these methods should be interpreted with caution. 

In conclusion, the assumed strain methods are a powerful tool in the field of computational mechanics of materials. They provide a simplified approach to solving complex problems, but their results should be validated against more rigorous methods whenever possible.

## Chapter: Chapter 11: Viscoelasticity

### Introduction

Viscoelasticity is a fascinating and complex field within the broader discipline of computational mechanics of materials. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of viscoelasticity, providing a comprehensive understanding of its principles and applications.

Viscoelastic materials exhibit both viscous and elastic characteristics when undergoing deformation. This behavior is often observed in polymers, biological tissues, and certain types of metals. Understanding viscoelasticity is crucial in many engineering and scientific disciplines, including biomechanics, materials science, and polymer processing.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe viscoelastic behavior. These models often involve differential equations, such as the Maxwell and Kelvin-Voigt models, which describe the response of a viscoelastic material to a step change in deformation. We will also discuss the concept of creep and stress relaxation, which are key to understanding the long-term behavior of viscoelastic materials.

We will also delve into the computational aspects of viscoelasticity. This includes the numerical methods used to solve the differential equations that describe viscoelastic behavior, as well as the use of finite element methods to model viscoelastic materials.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the principles of viscoelasticity, the mathematical models used to describe it, and the computational methods used to analyze it. This knowledge will provide a strong foundation for further study in this exciting and important field.




#### 10.6b Applications of Discretized Lagrangian in Elasticity

The discretized Lagrangian formulation is a powerful tool in the study of elasticity. It allows us to discretize the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solve the problem within each element using a set of basis functions. The solution within each element is then assembled to form the overall solution.

##### Finite Element Method in Elasticity

The Finite Element Method (FEM) is a numerical method that discretises the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solves the problem within each element using a set of basis functions. The solution within each element is then assembled to form the overall solution.

In the context of elasticity, the FEM can be combined with the gradient discretisation method (GDM) to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FEM is used to solve the problem within each element.

The implementation of the FEM involves discretising the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solving the system of equations that represents the problem within each element. This is typically done using a numerical solver, such as the Newton-Raphson method.

##### Finite Volume Method in Elasticity

The Finite Volume Method (FVM) is a numerical method that discretises the problem domain into a finite number of volumes, and then solves the problem within each volume using a set of basis functions. The solution within each volume is then assembled to form the overall solution.

In the context of elasticity, the FVM can be combined with the GDM to solve problems involving anisotropic materials. The GDM is used to approximate the strain in the material, while the FVM is used to solve the problem within each volume.

The implementation of the FVM involves discretising the problem domain into a finite number of volumes, and then solving the system of equations that represents the problem within each volume. This is typically done using a numerical solver, such as the Newton-Raphson method.

##### Discretized Lagrangian in Elasticity

The discretized Lagrangian formulation is particularly useful in the study of elasticity. It allows us to discretize the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solve the problem within each element using a set of basis functions. The solution within each element is then assembled to form the overall solution.

The implementation of the discretized Lagrangian formulation involves discretising the problem domain into a finite number of elements, and then solving the system of equations that represents the problem within each element. This is typically done using a numerical solver, such as the Newton-Raphson method.

The discretized Lagrangian formulation is a powerful tool in the study of elasticity, and its applications are vast. It is used in a variety of fields, including structural analysis, solid mechanics, and material science. Its ability to handle complex geometries and boundary conditions makes it a versatile tool in the study of elasticity.

## Chapter 11: Assumed Strain Methods:

This is where finite elasticity comes into play.

Finite elasticity is a branch of mechanics of materials that deals with the nonlinear behavior of materials under complex loading conditions. It is a crucial aspect of computational mechanics, as it allows us to accurately model and predict the behavior of materials in various engineering applications. This chapter will provide a comprehensive guide to finite elasticity, covering its principles, applications, and numerical methods for solving finite elasticity problems.

Next, we will explore the numerical methods for solving finite elasticity problems, including the finite element method and the boundary element method. These methods are essential tools for engineers and researchers, as they allow us to solve complex finite elasticity problems that cannot be solved analytically.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of finite elasticity and its applications in computational mechanics of materials. 


## Chapter 11: Finite Elasticity:




### Subsection: 11.1a Definition and Properties of Finite Elasticity

Finite elasticity is a branch of mechanics of materials that deals with the nonlinear behavior of materials under complex loading conditions. It is a crucial aspect of computational mechanics, as it allows us to accurately model and predict the behavior of materials in various engineering applications. In this section, we will define finite elasticity and discuss its properties.

#### Definition of Finite Elasticity

Finite elasticity is a mathematical model that describes the relationship between stress and strain in a material. Unlike linear elasticity, which assumes infinitesimal strains and linear relationships between stress and strain, finite elasticity takes into account the nonlinear behavior of materials under complex loading conditions. This allows us to accurately model and predict the behavior of materials in real-world applications.

#### Properties of Finite Elasticity

Finite elasticity has several key properties that make it a powerful tool in computational mechanics. These properties include:

1. Nonlinearity: Finite elasticity takes into account the nonlinear behavior of materials, which is often necessary to accurately model and predict the behavior of materials in real-world applications.
2. Complex loading conditions: Finite elasticity allows us to consider complex loading conditions, such as multiaxial stress states and time-varying loading, which are often present in engineering applications.
3. Accurate prediction of material behavior: By considering the nonlinear behavior of materials, finite elasticity allows us to accurately predict the behavior of materials in various engineering applications.
4. Numerical methods for solving complex problems: Finite elasticity is often solved using numerical methods, such as the finite element method and the boundary element method, which allow us to solve complex problems that cannot be solved analytically.

In the next section, we will delve into the different types of finite elasticity models and their applications in modeling the nonlinear behavior of materials.


## Chapter 1:1: Finite Elasticity:




### Subsection: 11.1b State of Stress in Finite Elastic Solids

In the previous section, we discussed the properties of finite elasticity and its importance in computational mechanics. In this section, we will delve deeper into the concept of stress in finite elastic solids.

#### Stress in Finite Elastic Solids

Stress is a fundamental concept in mechanics that describes the internal forces acting within a material. In finite elasticity, stress is defined as the force per unit area that a material experiences when subjected to external forces. It is a tensor quantity, meaning it has both magnitude and direction.

The state of stress in a finite elastic solid can be represented by a second-order tensor, known as the stress tensor. The stress tensor is a 3x3 matrix that relates the stress vector to the normal vector of a surface. It is defined as:

$$
\sigma_{ij} = \frac{F_i}{A_j}
$$

where $\sigma_{ij}$ is the stress tensor, $F_i$ is the force vector, and $A_j$ is the area vector.

#### Types of Stress in Finite Elastic Solids

There are three types of stress that can occur in a finite elastic solid: tensile stress, compressive stress, and shear stress. Tensile stress occurs when a material is being pulled apart, while compressive stress occurs when a material is being pushed together. Shear stress, on the other hand, occurs when two forces act parallel to each other but in opposite directions.

#### State of Stress in Finite Elastic Solids

The state of stress in a finite elastic solid can be represented by a stress matrix, which is a 3x3 matrix that relates the stress vector to the normal vector of a surface. The stress matrix is defined as:

$$
\sigma = \begin{bmatrix}
\sigma_{xx} & \sigma_{xy} & \sigma_{xz} \\
\sigma_{yx} & \sigma_{yy} & \sigma_{yz} \\
\sigma_{zx} & \sigma_{zy} & \sigma_{zz}
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $\sigma_{ij}$ is the stress tensor and $i,j$ represent the direction of the stress vector and normal vector, respectively.

#### Drained State of Stress

In some cases, it is necessary to consider the drained state of stress in a finite elastic solid. This occurs when the material is subjected to external forces while being immersed in a fluid. In this case, the stress matrix can be modified to include the effects of the fluid pressure, as shown below:

$$
\sigma = \begin{bmatrix}
\sigma_{xx} - \sigma_{hydrostatic} & \sigma_{xy} & \sigma_{xz} \\
\sigma_{yx} & \sigma_{yy} - \sigma_{hydrostatic} & \sigma_{yz} \\
\sigma_{zx} & \sigma_{zy} & \sigma_{zz} - \sigma_{hydrostatic}
\end{bmatrix} + \begin{bmatrix}
\sigma_{hydrostatic} & 0 & 0 \\
0 & \sigma_{hydrostatic} & 0 \\
0 & 0 & \sigma_{hydrostatic}
\end{bmatrix} + \begin{bmatrix}
0 & 0 & \sigma_{z} \\
0 & 0 & 0 \\
0 & 0 & \sigma_{z}
\end{bmatrix}
$$

where $\sigma_{hydrostatic}$ is the mean fluid pressure and $\sigma_{z}$ is the additional fluid pressure in the z-direction.

#### Conclusion

In this section, we have discussed the concept of stress in finite elastic solids and its importance in computational mechanics. We have also explored the different types of stress that can occur in a material and the state of stress in a finite elastic solid. In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of strain and its relationship with stress in finite elastic solids.





#### 11.2a Metric Tensor and Metric Changes

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of stress in finite elastic solids. In this section, we will explore the role of metric tensors in finite elasticity.

#### Metric Tensors in Finite Elasticity

A metric tensor is a mathematical object that describes the geometry of a space. In finite elasticity, the metric tensor is used to describe the deformation of a material under external forces. It is a second-order tensor that relates the infinitesimal elements of a material to their deformed counterparts.

The metric tensor is defined as:

$$
g_{ij} = \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial X_j}
$$

where $g_{ij}$ is the metric tensor, $x_i$ is the position vector of a point in the deformed configuration, and $X_j$ is the position vector of the same point in the undeformed configuration.

#### Metric Changes in Finite Elasticity

In finite elasticity, the metric tensor can change as a material deforms under external forces. This change in the metric tensor is known as a metric change. Metric changes can be represented by the deformation gradient tensor, which is a 3x3 matrix that relates the position vector of a point in the undeformed configuration to its position vector in the deformed configuration.

The deformation gradient tensor is defined as:

$$
F = \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial X_j}
$$

where $F$ is the deformation gradient tensor, $x_i$ is the position vector of a point in the deformed configuration, and $X_j$ is the position vector of the same point in the undeformed configuration.

#### Metric Changes and Finite Elasticity

In finite elasticity, metric changes play a crucial role in understanding the deformation of a material under external forces. They allow us to relate the infinitesimal elements of a material in the undeformed configuration to their deformed counterparts. This is essential in analyzing the stress and strain in a material, as well as predicting its behavior under different loading conditions.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of strain in finite elasticity and its relationship with metric changes.





#### 11.2b Metric Changes in Deformable Bodies

In the previous section, we discussed the role of metric tensors in finite elasticity. In this section, we will delve deeper into the concept of metric changes in deformable bodies.

#### Metric Changes in Deformable Bodies

In deformable bodies, the metric tensor can change as the body deforms under external forces. This change in the metric tensor is known as a metric change. Metric changes can be represented by the deformation gradient tensor, which is a 3x3 matrix that relates the position vector of a point in the undeformed configuration to its position vector in the deformed configuration.

The deformation gradient tensor is defined as:

$$
F = \frac{\partial x_i}{\partial X_j}
$$

where $F$ is the deformation gradient tensor, $x_i$ is the position vector of a point in the deformed configuration, and $X_j$ is the position vector of the same point in the undeformed configuration.

#### Metric Changes and Deformable Bodies

In deformable bodies, metric changes play a crucial role in understanding the deformation of the body under external forces. They allow us to relate the infinitesimal elements of the body in the undeformed configuration to their deformed counterparts. This is essential in analyzing the stress and strain in the body, as well as predicting its behavior under different loading conditions.

#### Metric Changes and Deformation Gradient Tensor

The deformation gradient tensor is a key component in understanding metric changes in deformable bodies. It is a 3x3 matrix that relates the position vector of a point in the undeformed configuration to its position vector in the deformed configuration. This tensor is crucial in understanding the deformation of a body under external forces.

The deformation gradient tensor can be used to calculate the strain tensor, which is a measure of the deformation of a body. The strain tensor is defined as:

$$
\epsilon = \frac{1}{2}(F + F^T - I)(F + F^T - I)^T
$$

where $F$ is the deformation gradient tensor, $I$ is the identity matrix, and $^T$ denotes the transpose of a matrix.

#### Metric Changes and Deformation Gradient Tensor

The deformation gradient tensor is also used in the calculation of the stress tensor, which is a measure of the internal forces within a body. The stress tensor is defined as:

$$
\sigma = C(\epsilon - \epsilon_0)
$$

where $C$ is the elastic modulus tensor, $\epsilon$ is the strain tensor, and $\epsilon_0$ is the initial strain tensor.

The deformation gradient tensor is also used in the calculation of the strain energy density function, which is a measure of the energy stored in a body due to deformation. The strain energy density function is defined as:

$$
W = \frac{1}{2}\epsilon:\sigma
$$

where $:\ $ denotes the double dot product of two tensors.

In conclusion, the deformation gradient tensor plays a crucial role in understanding metric changes in deformable bodies. It is used in the calculation of the strain tensor, stress tensor, and strain energy density function, which are all essential in analyzing the deformation and behavior of a body under external forces. 





#### 11.3a Field Equations: Linear Momentum Balance, Angular Momentum Balance, Energy Balance

In the previous sections, we have discussed the role of metric tensors and deformation gradient tensors in understanding the deformation of deformable bodies. In this section, we will explore the field equations that govern the behavior of these bodies.

#### Field Equations

Field equations are mathematical expressions that describe the behavior of a physical system. In the context of finite elasticity, these equations are derived from the principles of conservation of linear momentum, angular momentum, and energy. They are used to describe the response of a deformable body to external forces.

The field equations for finite elasticity can be written as:

$$
\rho \frac{D \mathbf{v}}{Dt} = \nabla \cdot \mathbf{\sigma} + \mathbf{b}
$$

$$
\rho \mathbf{v} \times \frac{D \mathbf{\omega}}{Dt} = \nabla \times \mathbf{\sigma} + \mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{v}
$$

$$
\rho \frac{D E}{Dt} = \nabla \cdot (\mathbf{\sigma} \cdot \mathbf{v}) + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{v}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the body, $\mathbf{v}$ is the velocity of the body, $\mathbf{\omega}$ is the angular velocity of the body, $\mathbf{\sigma}$ is the Cauchy stress tensor, $\mathbf{b}$ is the body force per unit volume, and $E$ is the total energy per unit volume.

#### Linear Momentum Balance

The linear momentum balance equation describes the conservation of linear momentum in a deformable body. It states that the rate of change of linear momentum of a body is equal to the sum of the forces acting on the body. This equation is derived from Newton's second law of motion.

#### Angular Momentum Balance

The angular momentum balance equation describes the conservation of angular momentum in a deformable body. It states that the rate of change of angular momentum of a body is equal to the sum of the torques acting on the body. This equation is derived from the principles of rotational dynamics.

#### Energy Balance

The energy balance equation describes the conservation of energy in a deformable body. It states that the rate of change of energy of a body is equal to the sum of the work done on the body and the heat added to the body. This equation is derived from the first law of thermodynamics.

In the next section, we will explore the applications of these field equations in the analysis of deformable bodies.

#### 11.3b Solutions to Field Equations

In this section, we will explore the solutions to the field equations derived in the previous section. These solutions will provide a deeper understanding of the behavior of deformable bodies under external forces.

#### Solutions to Linear Momentum Balance

The linear momentum balance equation can be solved by considering the forces acting on a deformable body. These forces can be categorized into two types: body forces and surface forces. Body forces, such as gravity or electromagnetic forces, act throughout the volume of the body. Surface forces, such as pressure or friction, act on the surface of the body.

The linear momentum balance equation can be written as:

$$
\rho \frac{D \mathbf{v}}{Dt} = \nabla \cdot \mathbf{\sigma} + \mathbf{b}
$$

This equation states that the rate of change of linear momentum of a body is equal to the divergence of the Cauchy stress tensor plus the body force per unit volume.

#### Solutions to Angular Momentum Balance

The angular momentum balance equation can be solved by considering the torques acting on a deformable body. These torques can be categorized into two types: body torques and surface torques. Body torques, such as moments of inertia, act throughout the volume of the body. Surface torques, such as friction or torsion, act on the surface of the body.

The angular momentum balance equation can be written as:

$$
\rho \mathbf{v} \times \frac{D \mathbf{\omega}}{Dt} = \nabla \times \mathbf{\sigma} + \mathbf{b} \times \mathbf{v}
$$

This equation states that the rate of change of angular momentum of a body is equal to the curl of the Cauchy stress tensor plus the cross product of the body force per unit volume and the velocity of the body.

#### Solutions to Energy Balance

The energy balance equation can be solved by considering the work done on a deformable body and the heat added to the body. The work done on the body can be calculated from the stress tensor and the velocity of the body. The heat added to the body can be calculated from the specific enthalpy of the body.

The energy balance equation can be written as:

$$
\rho \frac{D E}{Dt} = \nabla \cdot (\mathbf{\sigma} \cdot \mathbf{v}) + \mathbf{b} \cdot \mathbf{v}
$$

This equation states that the rate of change of energy of a body is equal to the divergence of the dot product of the stress tensor and the velocity of the body plus the dot product of the body force per unit volume and the velocity of the body.

In the next section, we will explore the applications of these solutions in the analysis of deformable bodies.

#### 11.3c Applications of Field Equations

In this section, we will explore the applications of the field equations in the analysis of deformable bodies. These applications will provide a practical understanding of the concepts discussed in the previous sections.

#### Applications of Linear Momentum Balance

The linear momentum balance equation is used to analyze the motion of deformable bodies under external forces. This equation is particularly useful in the study of mechanical systems, such as machines and structures.

For example, consider a simple pendulum. The pendulum is a deformable body subjected to a gravitational force. The linear momentum balance equation can be used to derive the equation of motion for the pendulum. This equation can then be used to analyze the behavior of the pendulum under different conditions, such as changes in the length of the pendulum or the strength of the gravitational force.

#### Applications of Angular Momentum Balance

The angular momentum balance equation is used to analyze the rotation of deformable bodies under external torques. This equation is particularly useful in the study of rotating machinery and structures.

For example, consider a rotating shaft. The shaft is a deformable body subjected to torques. The angular momentum balance equation can be used to derive the equation of motion for the shaft. This equation can then be used to analyze the behavior of the shaft under different conditions, such as changes in the torque or the speed of rotation.

#### Applications of Energy Balance

The energy balance equation is used to analyze the energy changes in deformable bodies under external forces and heat transfer. This equation is particularly useful in the study of thermal systems, such as engines and refrigerators.

For example, consider a steam engine. The engine is a deformable body subjected to external forces and heat transfer. The energy balance equation can be used to derive the equation of motion for the engine. This equation can then be used to analyze the behavior of the engine under different conditions, such as changes in the steam pressure or the heat transfer rate.

In the next section, we will explore the numerical methods used to solve the field equations.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of finite elasticity, a critical aspect of computational mechanics of materials. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the behavior of elastic materials under various conditions. The chapter has provided a comprehensive understanding of the mathematical models and computational techniques used to analyze the elastic properties of materials.

We have learned that finite elasticity is a branch of mechanics that deals with the deformation of elastic bodies under the action of external forces. It is a field that is crucial in the design and analysis of structures and machines, as it helps engineers understand how materials will respond to different loads.

The chapter has also introduced the concept of stress and strain, and how they are related to the deformation of materials. We have seen how these concepts are used to describe the mechanical properties of materials, and how they can be calculated using mathematical models.

In addition, we have explored the concept of elasticity tensor, a mathematical object that describes the elastic properties of a material. We have learned how to calculate the elasticity tensor for different types of materials, and how to use it to predict the behavior of materials under different conditions.

Finally, we have discussed the importance of computational methods in the study of finite elasticity. We have seen how computer programs can be used to solve complex problems in elasticity, and how they can help engineers design more efficient and reliable structures and machines.

In conclusion, finite elasticity is a complex but fascinating field that plays a crucial role in the design and analysis of structures and machines. By understanding the principles of finite elasticity and how to apply them using computational methods, engineers can design more efficient and reliable structures and machines.

#### Exercise 1
Calculate the stress tensor for a material under a uniaxial tensile load. Use the Hooke's law to describe the elastic properties of the material.

#### Exercise 2
A beam is subjected to a bending moment. Use the finite elasticity theory to calculate the stress distribution in the beam.

#### Exercise 3
A cylindrical pressure vessel is made of a material with isotropic elastic properties. Use the elasticity tensor to calculate the strain energy stored in the vessel under an internal pressure.

#### Exercise 4
Write a computer program to solve the finite elasticity problem for a cantilever beam under a distributed load. Use the finite difference method to discretize the beam and the Newton-Raphson method to solve the resulting system of equations.

#### Exercise 5
Discuss the limitations of the finite elasticity theory. How can these limitations be overcome?

## Chapter: Chapter 12: Viscoelasticity

### Introduction

Viscoelasticity is a fascinating field that combines the principles of both viscosity and elasticity. It is a property exhibited by certain materials, including polymers, biological tissues, and some metals, where they simultaneously exhibit both viscous and elastic characteristics when undergoing deformation. This chapter, "Viscoelasticity," will delve into the intricacies of this property, exploring its implications and applications in the realm of computational mechanics.

Viscoelastic materials are unique in that they can deform under stress and then slowly return to their original shape once the stress is removed. This behavior is a blend of the viscous property, which allows for continuous deformation under stress, and the elastic property, which allows for the material to return to its original shape once the stress is removed. This blend of properties makes viscoelastic materials particularly challenging to model and analyze, but also incredibly useful in a variety of applications.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe viscoelastic behavior, including the Maxwell and Kelvin-Voigt models. These models, expressed in terms of differential equations, provide a mathematical framework for understanding and predicting the behavior of viscoelastic materials. We will also discuss the numerical methods used to solve these equations, such as the finite difference method and the finite element method.

Furthermore, we will delve into the applications of viscoelasticity in computational mechanics. Viscoelastic materials are used in a wide range of applications, from the design of biomedical devices to the manufacturing of polymer-based products. Understanding the behavior of these materials under different loading conditions is crucial for the design and analysis of these applications.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the principles of viscoelasticity, the mathematical models used to describe it, and the numerical methods used to solve these models. They should also be able to apply this knowledge to the analysis of viscoelastic materials in various applications.
